    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer11192650796281223731">Connie Willis' Hugo and Nebula Award-winning Doomsday Book uses time travel for a serious look at how people connect with each other. In this Hugo-winning companion to that novel, she offers a completely different kind of time travel adventure: a delightful romantic comedy that pays hilarious homage to Jerome K. Jerome's Three Men in a Boat.<br/><br/>When too many jumps back to 1940</span>
  <span id="freeText11192650796281223731" style="display:none">Connie Willis' Hugo and Nebula Award-winning Doomsday Book uses time travel for a serious look at how people connect with each other. In this Hugo-winning companion to that novel, she offers a completely different kind of time travel adventure: a delightful romantic comedy that pays hilarious homage to Jerome K. Jerome's Three Men in a Boat.<br /><br />When too many jumps back to 1940 leave 21st century Oxford history student Ned Henry exhausted, a relaxing trip to Victorian England seems the perfect solution. But complexities like recalcitrant rowboats, missing cats, and love at first sight make Ned's holiday anything but restful - to say nothing of the way hideous pieces of Victorian art can jeopardize the entire course of history.</span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/7132145-gail-gibbs"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1442971695i/7132145._UX100_CR0,6,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/7132145-gail-gibbs">Gail Gibbs</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Actually, there are two books you don't have to read. The phrase "To say nothing of the dog" is a reference to Jerome K. Jerome's delightful travelog,<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Actually, there are two books you don't have to read. The phrase "To say nothing of the dog" is a reference to Jerome K. Jerome's delightful travelog, Three Men in a Boat, written in 1880's, and full of wry observations about his own Victorian society. Connie Willis also wrote Doomsday Book, with a similar theme and some of the same characters. Both are excellent, but To Say Nothing of the Dog stands alone nicely. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_110525734" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/110525734" />
    <a title="carol. " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3978225-carol"><img alt="carol. " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307934801p2/3978225.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/110525734?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carol. " class="user" itemprop="url" name="carol. " href="/user/show/3978225-carol">carol. </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=awards">awards</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=time-period-fantasy">time-period-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=desert-island">desert-island</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=my-library-hardcover">my-library-hardcover</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer110525734" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10831602388372633694">If ever there was a symphony as book (Beethoven's 8th?), it would be this one. Like a symphony, <u>To Say Nothing</u> is a wonderful composite that is almost impossible to deconstruct. In many books, there might be a chapter that stands out, whether due to brilliance or failure; this is largely a harmonious, excellently written whole, with only one or two incongruous passages near the end. <br/><br/>Then there's the writing: amazingly developed and interwoven, it takes a number of disparate themes and juxtaposes</span>
  <span id="freeText10831602388372633694" style="display:none">If ever there was a symphony as book (Beethoven's 8th?), it would be this one. Like a symphony, <u>To Say Nothing</u> is a wonderful composite that is almost impossible to deconstruct. In many books, there might be a chapter that stands out, whether due to brilliance or failure; this is largely a harmonious, excellently written whole, with only one or two incongruous passages near the end. <br /><br />Then there's the writing: amazingly developed and interwoven, it takes a number of disparate themes and juxtaposes them. Like a flute soaring above the rest of the orchestra, there are playful little giggles throughout, largely due to reoccurring motifs. Particular favorites include Ned's bemusement at hearing anarchoristic words (<b>"poppycock"</b> and <b>"drat"</b>), unfortunate couples that end in disaster, Ned's inability to read a Roman numeral pocket watch (<b>"I dozed off again at half past V"</b>) and the fickleness of cats. There are serious undertones, and a sense of urgency; the characters need to achieve their personal mission, but are also extremely concerned about their detrimental impact on history. And, to be completely honest, like a symphony, one needs to be in the mood and willing to pay attention, otherwise it just becomes so much soporific background noise.<br /><br />The almost-impossible summary: in the year 2057, Lady Schrapnell (is there a more perfectly named character?) has come to England, determined to rebuild Coventry Cathedral, where her exponentially-great grandmother experienced a life-changing event. In her zeal, she's determined to make every detail perfect (<b>"God is in the details"</b>) and has enlisted the Temporal Physics department of the University to make it happen. The story is told by temporal historian Ned Henry, who has most recently been in 1940, looking through the burned ruins of the Cathedral for the 'bishop's bird stump,' a hideous paragon to the lack of Victorian taste (<b>"It did, however, have twining ivy and a bas-relief of either Noah's ark or the battle of Jericho."</b>) His partner pulls him back to normal time when it is discovered he's suffering from time lag, evidenced by <b>"one of the first symptoms of time-lag is a tendency to maudlin sentimentality, like an Irishman in his cups or a Victorian poet cold-sober."</b> His interview in the Infirmary always makes me laugh (<b>"Infirmary nurses usually resemble something out of the Spanish Inquisition, but this one had an almost kindly face, the sort an assistant torturer... might have."</b>)<br /><br />Ned is sent to 1888 with the dual purpose of recovering in the pastoral Victorian English countryside and returning an object to 1888 restore an incongruity and preserve the historical timeline. He meets an Oxford undergrad, Terence, and takes a idyllic boat ride down the Thames with him, only to discover Terence is intent on meeting a new infatuation, Lady Schrapnell's great(s)-grandmother, Tossie. While she has not attained the bossy demeanor of Lady S., she nonetheless has almost everyone falling in line with her ridiculous plans that include a seance and a jumble sale. <br /><br />What follows is a comedy of errors as the time-traveling historians attempt to keep the young would-be lovers separated. The historians are convinced Tossie needs to fall in love with an unknown man with the initial 'C' and begin combing the countryside for eligible (and not-so-eligible) bachelors. Accompanying them is a genuine Oxford don distracted by fish and history, a tenacious and fierce bulldog named Cyril, and a black cat. As cats are extinct in the modern era, poor Ned is particularly unskilled in managing them:<br /><b><br />"I set her down, and she walked a few feet across the grass and then took off like a shot and disappeared round the corner of a wall. <br />I told you so, Cyril said.<br />"Well, don't just stand there. Go after her," I said.<br />Cyril continued sitting.<br />He had a point. Our chasing after her in the woods hadn't been a roaring success. "Well, what do you suggest then?"<br />He lay down, his muzzle against the milk bottle, and it wasn't a bad idea."<br /></b><br />A caveat: this is not hard or traditional science-fiction. The most science fiction-like aspect supposes that time travel is possible, but only in ways that don't effect the past or allow travelers to bring objects into the future. The field is known as temporal physics, and it while it is still being explored, incongruities--artificial changes to the timeline--could <b>"theoretically could alter the course of history, or if it were severe enough, destroy the universe."</b> Luckily for us, the universe is self-repairing, and has lines of defense that might manifest as an increase in coincidental events. We learn this in brief scenes between the time travelers and it's artfully done.<br /><br />Characterization is wonderfully done. The historians are well-developed and multi-dimensional. I confess I especially love Cyril, who is completely dog-like but provides a silent foil for Ned's thoughts.<br /><br />While I recognize the style and pace won't appeal to everyone, especially the action-adventure reader, I'm ridiculously fond of this book. I've re-read it numerous times, especially when I want to be in a book holding pattern, reading something familiar and enjoyable that didn't keep me up until 2 a.m. reading. I've read it so many times that I find myself quoting it, even if no one else gets my references. In fact, I once slightly embarrassed myself by exclaiming, <b>"a genuine Oxford don!"</b> courtesy of the passage, <b>"I sat there watching him examine the fish and marvelling at what we'd caught. A genuine eccentric Oxford don. They're an extinct species, too."</b> Well, he was a genuine eccentric don, after all--he studied voodoo and death practices.<br /><br />Anyone who reads my reviews knows I have a fondness for the well-turned phrase, but while I often smile reading this book, the humor is built up over repeating passages rather than the the standard quip. This is gentle, suspenseful, silly, romantic and sophisticated reading. Filled with literary references and philosophizing on the importance of individuals in history versus scientific principles, someone with a classic background might best appreciate the wide-ranging references, but despite my own infirm education, I didn't find them inaccessible. If you enjoy Bertie Wooster, Shakespeare, Agatha Christie and Lord Peter mysteries, history, gentle comedic romance and literary references, the sly wit in this book will keep you entertained. <br /><br />Cross posted at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2012/12/27/to-say-nothing-of-the-dog-by-connie-willis/">http://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2012/1...</a><br /><br />Re-read March, 2016</span>
  <a data-text-id="10831602388372633694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1291241742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16803189184106236104">· <b><u>Previous rating</u>: 5 stars</b> *eyerolls at her 2015 Self of Despicable Book Taste and Total Lack of Judgement*<br/>· <b><u>New rating</u>: 20 million stars</b>. <i>And a half.</i><br/><br/>➽ <b><u>And the moral of this rerererererereread is</u>:</b> Connie Willis’ <b>amazingly clever writing</b> + one of the <b>most beautifully constructed tale</b> ever + the <b>top ranking boyfriend</b> in my High Security Harem (aka Cyril the Bulldog) + <b>time travel</b> + <s>restful Victorian times</s> delightfully wacky <b>Victorian escapades</b> + <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7963.P_G_Wodehouse' rel='nofollow noopener'>PG Wodehouse</a> + awesome <b>space-time continuum incongru</b></span>
  <span id="freeText16803189184106236104" style="display:none">· <b><u>Previous rating</u>: 5 stars</b> *eyerolls at her 2015 Self of Despicable Book Taste and Total Lack of Judgement*<br>· <b><u>New rating</u>: 20 million stars</b>. <i>And a half.</i><br><br>➽ <b><u>And the moral of this rerererererereread is</u>:</b> Connie Willis’ <b>amazingly clever writing</b> + one of the <b>most beautifully constructed tale</b> ever + the <b>top ranking boyfriend</b> in my High Security Harem (aka Cyril the Bulldog) + <b>time travel</b> + <s>restful Victorian times</s> delightfully wacky <b>Victorian escapades</b> + <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7963.P_G_Wodehouse" rel="nofollow noopener">PG Wodehouse</a> + awesome <b>space-time continuum incongruities</b> and <b>self-corrections</b> and stuff + <b>hahahahaha </b> + the very aptly named <b>Lady Shrapnell</b> + <i>Difficulty Distinguishing Sounds</i> + <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">Agatha Christie</a> and my boyfriend Hercule Poirot + exotic <b>Japanese goldfish</b> (or lack thereof) + high explosive <b>bombs</b> + deliciously eccentric <b>Oxford dons</b> + calamities who look like <b>naiads</b> (and vice-versa) + <i>Slowness in Answering</i> + infirmary <b>nurses</b> and the <b>Spanish Inquisition</b> + the regulations of <b>animals in bed</b> + aged <b>relicts</b> (and not-so-aged ones, too) + <b>Ultra</b> + <i>Maudlin Sentimentality</i> + eating <b>vile, unspeakable</b> Victorian things for breakfast + the <b>Blitz</b> + <b>Couples That Come To A Bad End</b> + <i>naughty bad doggums vs. deawest, dearum, pwecious Juju</i> + <b>cats and cabs and fans, oh my</b> + <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8734.Dorothy_L_Sayers" rel="nofollow noopener">Dorothy L. Sayers</a> and Lord Peter Wimsey + slightly exasperating, <b>spoiled Victorian brats</b>+ <i>Disorientation</i> + Mrs. Chattisbourne’s <b>ever-giggling flower garden</b> + the <b>Enigma</b> machine + cat-induced <b>suffocation</b> + Napoleon’s <b>hemorrhoids</b> + butler <b>pinching</b> + <b>chaos theory</b> + <i>the bird Luftwaffe</i> + the somewhat hideous <b>bishop’s bird stump</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <i>duh</i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> + I could go on and on but you might as well <b>read the book</b>, it might prove a little bit more <b>time-efficient</b> and stuff =<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1539157473ra/26427254.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>This is me, disguised as Cyril the Bulldog and being deliriously happy. Just so you know.</i><br><br>➽ <b><u>And the <i>other</i> moral of this rerererererereread is</u>:</b> I <b>really suck</b> at reviewing this book. QED and stuff.<br><br><b>P.S.</b> The best review you will ever read for this book is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/110525734" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. You are quiet welcome, Comely Decapods Mine.<br><b>P.P.S.</b> <a href="http://froogal.blogspot.com/2010/10/mystery-of-pen-wiper.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Penwiper</a>, anyone?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479540926i/21203689._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>[<b>Pre-non-review nonsense</b>]<br><br><b>Review to come</b> and stuff. Oh, may I leave <b>Cyril the Bulldog</b> with you while I write it? He's really super cool, I promise.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1539020221ra/26418521.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>See what I mean</b>? Coolest dog ever. He also happens to be <b>Mostest Awesomest Literary Dog That Ever Was And Ever Will Be</b> (MALDTEWAEWB™), so you better take good care of him while I'm away. But hey, no pressure and stuff. It's not like I'll <b>unleash the murderous crustaceans</b> on you if you suck at baby-sitting him and stuff.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479540926i/21203689._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>[<b>May 2015</b>]<br><br><b>Surprising as it may be</b>, this is not going to be one of my overly formatted, nonsensical, gif-filled reviews. This will be a long, very boring review that will not do this book justice. Quite a few digressions to be expected, too. There will be no silly fangirling involved either. <b>My love for this book goes beyond that</b>. <br><br><b>If I had to choose 3 books</b> to take with me to a desert island this would be one of them. I love it more with every reread. Why? Because it's the improbable mix of three of the things I love the most. <b>And as improbable as the mix is, it works</b>. Because Connie Willis is an amazing writer. No, strike that. Simply saying she's an amazing writer doesn't even begin to cover it. There are not enough words to express how much I love her and every book she ever wrote. I could reread each and every one of them over and over again and never get bored (except maybe from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7519231-all-clear" rel="nofollow noopener">All Clear</a>, which was the sad exception to my I'm-Desperately-In-Love-With-Anything-Connie-Willis-Ever-Wrote-Writes-And-Will-Write condition). <b>To get to the point: Connie Willis = Sheer Brilliance</b> <i>← yes, I lied when I said there would be no fangirling to be had in this review. It might not be silly but it's there.</i><br><br><b>At this point you might</b> be wondering what that improbable mix of three of the things I love the most I mentioned above is. Then again, probably not. But hey, I started writing this review so I might as well get on with it. And <b>I guess I should issue a warning at this point</b>, because I'm going to talk about my grandmother. Yes, my grandmother. I never get personal in my reviews but this book wouldn't be one of my all-time favourites had it not been for my grandmother. So I want to tell you a few things about her. Because she's the one who introduced me to those <b>Famous Three Things that make me love this book so much</b>. <br><br><b>My grandmother</b> was French Canadian. She was born in 1910 and stayed in love with the 1930s and 1940s of her youth until her dying day. The movies, the stars, the fashion, she loved it all. When I was growing up she had me watch all the screwball comedies she could get her hands on. <b>She had tons of 1930s &amp; 1940s movies on VHS tapes</b> (yes, I am aware that 90% of GR users probably don't even know what those are. It sucks to be revoltingly young, doesn’t it?) and I would watch them over and over again. And used to wish I'd not been born in the silly 1970s. (<b>The Love Boat and disco vs. George Cukor and Cary Grant? Please someone take me back in time posthaste</b>.) And I wanted to be Katharine Hepburn in <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0032904/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Philadelphia Story</a> when I grew up. So that's Famous Three Things #1: <b>screwball comedies</b>.<br><br><b>Back to my grandmother</b>. She was crazy about British Literature. And when I say crazy I mean CRAZY. She loved the classics but the one thing she just couldn't resist? Humor and wit. The first book she ever made me read? <b><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18025.Something_Fresh" rel="nofollow noopener">Something Fresh</a> by P.G. Wodehouse</b>. I can't remember how old I was when I first read it but I do remember falling in love with Wodehouse. He's been one of my favorite authors ever since. My grandmother's 1933 copy of Something Fresh sits on my bedside table and whenever I feel down I just open it at random and read a few pages. <b>It works better than all the alcohol in the world</b>. <br><br><b>Now there was one book</b> that my grandmother suggested I read that I've always had mixed feelings about: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men in a Boat</a> by Jerome K. Jerome. Why is this important? <b>Because the subtitle for this much-loved classic is To Say Nothing of the Dog</b>. Yes, this is where Connie Willis got the title for her book. Jerome's story plays a very important part in Willis' book, too. And Willis' book could be considered a homage to Jerome's. I can't recount how many times I've read Three Men in a Boat <b>in a desperate attempt to love it as much as everyone else does</b>. Sometimes I think it's boring as hell, sometimes I think "hey, I think I get it now" but most times <b>I just find it meh</b>. Now that I've just reread To Say Nothing of the Dog I feel like giving Jerome's book another try. Obviously. I wonder how it will go this time. I'm not keeping my hopes up. Anyway, this was Famous Three Things #2: <b>humorous British Lit</b>.<br><br><b>As much as she loved the 1930s</b> and British Lit, my grandmother was a science-fiction buff. Yeah, she was a pretty cool grandma. Her science fiction books? I haven't seen a more extensive collection to this day. <b>And although she didn't actually introduce me to time travel novels</b>, it's my grandmother's love for anything science-fiction that led me to discover that particular subgenre. <b>I was never a fan of space operas</b> and books involving aliens, distant galaxies, androids etc but time travel? I've always LOVED it (digression time: want to read one of the best time travel stories ever written? Read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40526.Time_and_Again" rel="nofollow noopener">Time and Again</a>. End of digression time). And that's Famous Three Things #3: <b>time travel</b>.<br><br><b>So I'm halfway through this review and I haven't said a thing about the book yet. Sigh. I told you this would be long and boring</b>. <br><br><b>Let's do a little recap here</b>. We have screwball comedies + humorous British Lit + time travel. And where does that take us? To Connie Willis of course. <b>Because To Say Nothing of the Dog is the perfect mix of all these things. And more. So much more</b>: it's P.G. Wodehouse meets the <b>space time continuum</b> meets comedic romp meets Agatha Christie meets the Victorian era meets Alternate History meets Dorothy L. Sayers meets fantasy of manners meets <b>Everything I've Ever Loved</b>. It's hilarious (well my kind of hilarious anyway), it's clever, it's witty, it's interesting, it's fun, it's thought-provoking and <b>DAMN I still haven't said a word about the story</b>. And I won't either. Just know that this book is about time travel and Victorian society. <b>About chaos theory and bulldogs</b>. About The Battle of Waterloo and hemorrhoids. About the butterfly effect and butlers who read Darwin. <b>About crisis points and the drowning of extinct cats</b>. About the Enigma machine and jumble sales. About penwipers and the least likely suspect. About Ultra and spiritualism. About time-lag and chaperones. <b>About meddling with history and loose lips</b>. About self-corrections and Coventry Cathedral. About space time continuum incongruities and Oxford dons. <b>And ultimately it's about the Bishop's bird stump</b>. But is it really? Maybe not. Everything is relative here. So you'll just have to read the book to find out. <b>If you still feel like reading</b> this book after being put through my review that is. I'm pretty sure the number of people who had this book on their to-read list <b>just plummeted</b>. So just forget you ever read this review and <b>trust me when I say: this book. Read it</b>.<br><br><i>Sadly enough my grandmother passed away before To Say Nothing of the Dog was published. Had she had the chance to read it I'm pretty sure she would have LOVED it as much as I do.</i></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="16803189184106236104" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2918324286777421314">Fateful re-read 5/4/18 <br/><br/>This is one of my all-time favorite books. From the clever phrases and deep PTSD exasperation to the total eventual collapse of the space-time continuum because of a freaking cat to THE BISHOP'S BIRD-STUMP, I find myself chortling nearly twenty years after the first read and again on the re-read.<br/><br/>We're catapulted through time thanks to the Oxford History Department's time machine put to the disposal of a wealthy American patron who is, let's be frank, NUTS. She's sent seemi</span>
  <span id="freeText2918324286777421314" style="display:none">Fateful re-read 5/4/18 <br /><br />This is one of my all-time favorite books. From the clever phrases and deep PTSD exasperation to the total eventual collapse of the space-time continuum because of a freaking cat to THE BISHOP'S BIRD-STUMP, I find myself chortling nearly twenty years after the first read and again on the re-read.<br /><br />We're catapulted through time thanks to the Oxford History Department's time machine put to the disposal of a wealthy American patron who is, let's be frank, NUTS. She's sent seemingly countless overworked historians into the Blitz to recover artifacts from the destroyed cathedral at Coventry. <br /><br />What really happens is a LOT of slippage in the time-stream, a deep mystery, even more miscommunication and strange coincidences and classic slapstick and some of the funniest Victorian Romance I've come across. <br /><br />Oh, it's definitely hardcore SF, but it's also a tribute to Jerome K Jerome's Three Men in a Boat and the spirit is very much alive and well.<br /><br />What we've got is a genre-masher of epic proportions. It's a high-stakes time-continuum travel and looming disaster, a truly atrocious MacGuffin that has everyone running around like headless chickens in a slapstick comedy, and a classic 1930's Hercule Peroit Agatha Christie mystery. <br /><br />All three genres are pulled off wonderfully! And she tops it all off with VERY well-turned phrases that stick with you so warmly. :) <br /><br />Charming? Beyond charming. Utterly delightful. No poppycock. :)</span>

  <span id="freeText6234030092017433183" style="display:none">Oh, dear. Every time I see the title of this book it makes me feel anxious.           </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer326278294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8123752828974173036"><a href='http://www.theaceofclouds.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>Christmas 2010: I realised that I had got stuck in a rut. I was re-reading old favourites again and again, waiting for a few trusted authors to release new works. Something had to be done.<br/><br/>On the spur of the moment I set myself a challenge, to read every book to have won the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2' rel='nofollow noopener'>Locus Sci-Fi</a> award. That’s 35 books, 6 of which I’d previously read, leaving 29 titles by 14 authors who were new to me.<br/><br/>While working through this reading list I got married, went on my honeymoon, switched career and beca</b></span>
  <span id="freeText8123752828974173036" style="display:none"><a href="http://www.theaceofclouds.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Christmas 2010: I realised that I had got stuck in a rut. I was re-reading old favourites again and again, waiting for a few trusted authors to release new works. Something had to be done.<br><br>On the spur of the moment I set myself a challenge, to read every book to have won the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2" rel="nofollow noopener">Locus Sci-Fi</a> award. That’s 35 books, 6 of which I’d previously read, leaving 29 titles by 14 authors who were new to me.<br><br>While working through this reading list I got married, went on my honeymoon, switched career and became a father. As such these stories became imprinted on my memory as the soundtrack to the happiest period in my life (so far).</b><br><br>Have you read <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24984.Passage" rel="nofollow noopener">Passage</a>
</i>, by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Connie Willis</a>? <br>I have. It was the first <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Willis</a> I read. <br>We didn’t get on.<br><br><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog" rel="nofollow noopener">To Say Nothing of the Dog</a>
</i> arrived in the post shortly afterwards, and I wasn’t exactly bouncing off the walls in excitement. I looked at the book. The book looked at me. I shoved in on a shelf and went back to reading <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/834518.Cyteen" rel="nofollow noopener">Cyteen</a>
</i>. I read another half-dozen books before I worked up the courage to even consider it.<br><br>I was packing books for my honeymoon. I wasn’t sure how much reading I’d get done because we were planning an action packed holiday (wink-wink-nudge-nudge!) so I lobbed in a couple of books I’d been putting off – ‘<i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dog</a>
</i>, and <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2723.The_Years_of_Rice_and_Salt" rel="nofollow noopener">Years of Rice &amp; Salt</a>
</i>. As it turned out, I nearly broke my ankle on our second day in Malta so we spent our time hobbling around the pool (and restaurants!) instead of all the mountain climbing and scuba-diving we had intended. Lots more time for reading than expected!<br><br>My darling wife actually nabbed '<i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dog</a>
</i> before I’d looked at it. I was still grinding my way through the latter half of <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/816.Cryptonomicon" rel="nofollow noopener">Cryptonomicon</a>
</i> when she started flicking through the first chapter.<br><br>Isn’t this by the same writer you called ‘bloody miserable’?<br><b>Yeah.</b><br>It’s funny!<br><b>Really?</b><br>Yeah.<br><b>...Really?</b><br>I’m borrowing it.<br><b>‘Kay.</b><br><br>It turned out she really enjoyed it and recommended it highly. Which came as something of a surprise. <br><br>My point (and I understand if you’d given up all hope of me reaching it) is that stories take you on a journey – and how you connect with and take enjoyment from that journey can be hugely influenced by what you expect from the book and what’s going on in your life at that time. Had I read ‘<i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dog</a>
</i> the moment it came through the letterbox (ignoring the fact that I would have been interrupting <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/834518.Cyteen" rel="nofollow noopener">Cyteen</a>
</i> midway through which is most out of character for me) I don’t think it would have had the same effect on the post-<i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24984.Passage" rel="nofollow noopener">Passage</a>
</i>, pre-Wedding, collapsing quantum wave-front that we call my sentient consciousness. But this was the right book at the right time, and I loved it!<br><br>With humour, it’s all subjective – it won’t be everyone’s cup of tea but I found it very funny. It’s not exactly laugh-out-loud funny, but definitely dry-tickle-in-your-cheeks funny and stick-in-your-head funny. Particularly the effects of time lag in ‘difficulty distinguishing sounds’ – this has become a long running gag in our house ever since. Pretending to have difficulty distinguishing what your wife has said when she’s asking you to do chores – it’s hilarious… to me anyway.<br><br>The cats. The dog. The goldfish. The chaos theory. The Alice-in-Wonderland conversations. The Bishop’s bloody bird-stump! Oh yes, please! More?<br><br>Never before has an author pulled a U-turn in my estimations with such panache; you wily bird, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Willis</a>! The <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/52985-oxford-time-travel" rel="nofollow noopener">Oxford Time-Travel</a> series is an odd and scatter-shot series. This book made me smile, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book" rel="nofollow noopener">Doomsday</a>
</i> tried hard to make me cry and <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6506307-blackout" rel="nofollow noopener">Blackout</a> / <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7519231-all-clear" rel="nofollow noopener">All Clear</a></i> had me tearing out my hair with anxiety. Why couldn’t she just write a direct sequel to this?<br><br>Ned gets the girl…<br>“And kissed her for a hundred and sixty-nine years.” <br> … but what happened next, hmmm!?<br><br>There should be a whole series of Victorian adventures with Ned and Verity. <br>And kittens. <br><br>A happy 5-stars. Definitely check it out.<br>You might not click – I’ll admit, it doesn’t work for a decent percentage of readers – but…but… you might just discover a new book for your favourites shelf. <br>It’s worth the risk, read it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8123752828974173036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3155090400187007813">$2.99 Kindle sale, Dec. 11, 2018. While this offbeat time-travel novel is a sequel of sorts to <i>Doomsday Book</i>, they have <u>completely</u> different vibes, and it's not really necessary to have read Doomsday Book before this one. This is one of my favorite books in the world, but it's kind of an odd one that probably won't work for everyone. It's a little bit madcap farce, with people running and time-hopping around trying to find some obscure, ugly piece of Victorian art; it's got a bit of romantic com</span>
  <span id="freeText3155090400187007813" style="display:none">$2.99 Kindle sale, Dec. 11, 2018. While this offbeat time-travel novel is a sequel of sorts to <i>Doomsday Book</i>, they have <u>completely</u> different vibes, and it's not really necessary to have read Doomsday Book before this one. This is one of my favorite books in the world, but it's kind of an odd one that probably won't work for everyone. It's a little bit madcap farce, with people running and time-hopping around trying to find some obscure, ugly piece of Victorian art; it's got a bit of romantic comedy, a bit of mystery, and a lot of this and that kind of all mashed together in a way that can seem confusing at times, but in the end is ABSOLUTELY BRILLIANT.<br /><br />Give it a shot. That's all.</span>

<div id="review_393345676" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/393345676" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4542979-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454402751p2/4542979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/393345676?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/4542979-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer393345676" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6976337335784900097"><br/>Two weeks ago I'd not heard of Connie Willis or of this novel. It came into my life because I randomly clicked through to <a href='http://www.theguardian.com/books/2010/sep/24/val-mcdermid-top-10-oxford-novels' rel='nofollow noopener'>this article</a> in <i>The Guardian</i> when I was looking for something completely different. Had I done my random clicking pre-Goodreads, I may well have passed on this novel, because "science-fiction fantasy" does not describe the kind of novel I generally read. But these days I'm much more adventurous, so I jumped right in. <br/><br/>What fun this was! It's a time travel story that (sort of) </span>
  <span id="freeText6976337335784900097" style="display:none"><br />Two weeks ago I'd not heard of Connie Willis or of this novel. It came into my life because I randomly clicked through to <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/books/2010/sep/24/val-mcdermid-top-10-oxford-novels" rel="nofollow noopener">this article</a> in <i>The Guardian</i> when I was looking for something completely different. Had I done my random clicking pre-Goodreads, I may well have passed on this novel, because "science-fiction fantasy" does not describe the kind of novel I generally read. But these days I'm much more adventurous, so I jumped right in. <br /><br />What fun this was! It's a time travel story that (sort of) makes sense, a farce, a romp, a screwball comedy, a comedy of manners, a romance, a mystery and a homage to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat" title="Three Men in a Boat by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men in a Boat</a>. It includes some truly hideous Victoriana, the burning of Coventry Cathedral, a dog and a cat, an eccentric Oxford don, a boat, a butler and allusions to writers including Shakespeare, Tennyson, Agatha Christie, Lewis Carroll, and Dorothy L Sayers (which was particularly special for me, because I'm a Dorothy L Sayers nut!). There was even a reference to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21787.The_Princess_Bride_" title="The Princess Bride  by William Goldman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Princess Bride</a>, or at least I think there was. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ A character saying "As you wish" more than once to a young lady struck me as a clue.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I absolutely loved reading this, all the more so because I took the advice of some GR reviewers and read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat" title="Three Men in a Boat by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men in a Boat</a> first. It's not essential to do so, but I'm very glad I did. This was the ideal novel to read while on holidays and I want to read it again. Here's to serendipitous random clicking, <i>The Guardian</i> and the expansion of literary boundaries brought to me courtesy of Goodreads. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6976337335784900097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1209800-colleen-the-convivial-curmudgeon?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1209800-colleen-the-convivial-curmudgeon?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1209800-colleen-the-convivial-curmudgeon?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer55066856" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10604929316214624374">2 1/2<br/><br/>I picked up this book because it was the read of the month for the Sci-fi/Fantasy group here on goodreads. Time travel tends to hurt my head, and this was no exception, but that's not what dragged it down.<br/><br/>Honestly, I can't quite put my finger on why I didn't like it - but I'll try.<br/><br/>To start with, I felt like it took a long while for the book to actually start. I guess there was too much set-up, or it was belabored too much. I didn't feel like it really got going until well into 200+ pages.<br/><br/>An</span>
  <span id="freeText10604929316214624374" style="display:none">2 1/2<br /><br />I picked up this book because it was the read of the month for the Sci-fi/Fantasy group here on goodreads. Time travel tends to hurt my head, and this was no exception, but that's not what dragged it down.<br /><br />Honestly, I can't quite put my finger on why I didn't like it - but I'll try.<br /><br />To start with, I felt like it took a long while for the book to actually start. I guess there was too much set-up, or it was belabored too much. I didn't feel like it really got going until well into 200+ pages.<br /><br />Another thing was that I just didn't get how this book was "hilarious". It was mildly amusing, what with the comedy of errors and the little snark on Victorian mannerisms, but it was also belabored to the point of being repetitive and overdone.<br /><br />I kept reading because I wanted to finish it, not really because I was compelled to keep reading. I was curious as to how it would all work out - of course I was never in any doubt that it would all work out somehow.<br /><br />I give it an extra half star because I like how the end sort of wrapped up everything nicely, while at the same time leaving a little dangling question, and I like how the romance between Verity and Ned was handled in juxtaposition to the saccharine Tossie and St. Trewes thing. Oh, and I did like the part with Baine, and Tossie, and the river... <br /><br />So 2 stars overall... 2 1/2 for a satisfying conclusion... </span>
  <a data-text-id="10604929316214624374" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer204871434" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16208486304603787596">A most entertaining adventure where Oxford dons get to meddle with time travel and a chance for the author to exercise her wit and to pay homage to great British authors. Everything is thrown into the pot - from ancient Greek battles to the decisions that sealed the fate of Napoleon at Waterloo, from Shakespeare to Tennyson, G K Chesterton to P G Wodehouse, Victorian morals and artistic expressions, boating on the Thames or the raid that destroyed the Coventry Cathedral in World War II, Lord Pet</span>
  <span id="freeText16208486304603787596" style="display:none">A most entertaining adventure where Oxford dons get to meddle with time travel and a chance for the author to exercise her wit and to pay homage to great British authors. Everything is thrown into the pot - from ancient Greek battles to the decisions that sealed the fate of Napoleon at Waterloo, from Shakespeare to Tennyson, G K Chesterton to P G Wodehouse, Victorian morals and artistic expressions, boating on the Thames or the raid that destroyed the Coventry Cathedral in World War II, Lord Peter Whimsey or Hercule Poirot , jumble sales or spiritualism . . . to say nothing of the dog (or the cat)<br />I couldn't stop laughing as I followed the well intended but clueless attempts of a pair of young historians to fix the time travel paradox of an errant cat and to discover the whereabouts of the infamous Bishop's Bird Stump.<br /> <br />I can understand how the numerous academic insider jokes and references to British culture and history can put off some readers, but I have always been a fan of some of the names mentioned above, so this book was exactly what I needed to put me in a good mood.<br /><br />[edit] spelling</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_295210207" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/295210207" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/295210207?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer295210207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14533118314839062768">We all like a good laugh don’t we? But for me, comedy works best in TV shows or movies. Humour in print works best in shorter formats, like cartoon strips or magazine articles. I tend to find “comic novels” (not to be confused with graphic novels) problematical. The trouble is I keep expecting to laugh at every page and that is a tall order for the authors. I don’t expect to be thrilled by every page of a thriller or to be scared by every page of a horror novel so I don’t know why I have such a </span>
  <span id="freeText14533118314839062768" style="display:none">We all like a good laugh don’t we? But for me, comedy works best in TV shows or movies. Humour in print works best in shorter formats, like cartoon strips or magazine articles. I tend to find “comic novels” (not to be confused with graphic novels) problematical. The trouble is I keep expecting to laugh at every page and that is a tall order for the authors. I don’t expect to be thrilled by every page of a thriller or to be scared by every page of a horror novel so I don’t know why I have such a high expectation of comic novels. Just a personal quirk I guess. Consequently, I tend to be less interested in comic novels because I find very few of them consistently funny through out the book.<br /><br /><i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> is a comic sci-fi novel, and it is a good one. It is not in the same league as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4.Douglas_Adams" title="Douglas Adams" rel="nofollow noopener">Douglas Adams</a><br />or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8489.Robert_Sheckley" title="Robert Sheckley" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert Sheckley</a> mind bendingly funny sf but it is a pleasant read and the humour works well enough from time to time. The style of humour is reminiscent of classic comic novels by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7963.P_G__Wodehouse" title="P.G. Wodehouse" rel="nofollow noopener">P.G. Wodehouse</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3565.Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde" rel="nofollow noopener">Oscar Wilde</a>. Of course the title of the book is a tribute to Jerome K. Jerome’s classic <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat__Three_Men___1_" title="Three Men in a Boat (Three Men, #1) by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men in a Boat</a>, a book I kind of dislike <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/541370983" rel="nofollow noopener">(review)</a>. I found it to be tedious, tame and almost mirth-free. Probably not Jerome’s fault it just did not work for me. Having read “Three Men” out of curiosity and as a sort of preparation for reading <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> my subsequent dislike of it does not bode well for Connie Willis’ book. On the other hand, I totally love her <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book__Oxford_Time_Travel___1_" title="Doomsday Book (Oxford Time Travel, #1) by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Doomsday Book</a>, one of the finest sf books I read in the past few years. <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> is part of Ms Willis' loosely connected <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/52985-oxford-time-travel" rel="nofollow noopener">Oxford Time Travel series</a> which includes <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book__Oxford_Time_Travel___1_" title="Doomsday Book (Oxford Time Travel, #1) by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Doomsday Book</a> but the tone is very different. While Doomsday Book is intense and tragic <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> is almost entirely breezy. I persevered through the less than riveting first few chapters and eventually settled into enjoying the book.<br /><br />It would be a mistake to expect <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> to be a sci-fi version of or tribute to Jerome’s book. Ms. Willis is clearly influenced by more diverse material than just one book. Her love for the crime fiction of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie" title="Agatha Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">Agatha Christie</a><br />And <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8734.Dorothy_L__Sayers" title="Dorothy L. Sayers" rel="nofollow noopener">Dorothy L. Sayers</a> is also evident. Best of all she did not neglect the sci-fi aspect of it, the book went on to win the Hugo and Locus Awards in 1999, and also a Nebula Award nomination.<br /><br />What raises <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> far above <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat__Three_Men___1_" title="Three Men in a Boat (Three Men, #1) by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men in a Boat</a> is that there is more to it than just trying to elicit laughter. There are the intriguing logic and logistics of time travelling which Willis beautifully worked out. The characters are also generally amiable and as something of an anglophile myself I enjoy the British culture references like jumble sales, the “tube trains” and the Jeevesian acerbic butler dialogue.<br /><br />My only complaint is that for the most part there is very little sense of urgency to the proceeding (until the last two or three chapters). The lighthearted tone is maintained throughout the book and the story moves amiably at a leisurely pace. This led to my initial feeling that the novel is too tame, the stakes are too low. As it turns out all of time and space continuum is at stake and a sense of danger eventually appears toward the end as the main characters’ strive to repair “incongruities”, which is Willis’ term for time travelling paradoxes. I like that she is using a different term for these paradoxes from the standard time travelling stories it somehow makes the story seem more believable.<br /><br /><i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> is not a laugh-a-minute book, it is not a complete success as a comic novel, but neither is it a failure. More importantly, as a lighthearted time travelling sci-fi novel it is worth a read. Just don’t go into it with the wrong expectations.<br /><br />(3.5 stars)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14533118314839062768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2377587539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2377587539" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2377587539?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8194647-to-say-nothing-of-the-dog">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2377587539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15940227782171329224">This is the second story about the Oxford Time Travel Institute. It is only loosely connected to the first, namely through Mr. Dunworthy, whom we know from book 1. And wasn't I glad about that (the first book and I didn't get along too splendidly).<br/><br/>It is 2057 (thus, a few years after the first book) and here, Mrs. Schrapnell, a rich and very eccentric (if not to say gaga) American lady is pouring money into rebuilding Coventry Cathedral exactly as it was before the Nazi Blitz during WWII. She has</span>
  <span id="freeText15940227782171329224" style="display:none">This is the second story about the Oxford Time Travel Institute. It is only loosely connected to the first, namely through Mr. Dunworthy, whom we know from book 1. And wasn't I glad about that (the first book and I didn't get along too splendidly).<br /><br />It is 2057 (thus, a few years after the first book) and here, Mrs. Schrapnell, a rich and very eccentric (if not to say gaga) American lady is pouring money into rebuilding Coventry Cathedral exactly as it was before the Nazi Blitz during WWII. She has therefore conscripted the Oxford history department to help her and, in addition, to find the Bishop's Bird Stump, a decorational piece from Coventry Cathedral. <br />The problem is that she’s working everyone to death, sending the historians up and down the timeline to find the blasted thing because it was lost (not destroyed, that much they know). But even if they find it (despite not even knowing what it looks like), they cannot just bring it to the future with them because the natural laws of the time continuum prevent anything of significance from being brought from the past to the future (or getting too close to historical events of significance so no historian can mess about). Any attempt at circumventing this law results in the time traveller either being sent to the right time but a completely different place (could be just a couple of kilometers or even another continent), or to the right place but the wrong time so the traveller can't create a paradox. In extreme cases, the continuum can even correct paradoxes by changing the course of events in minor ways to keep the original outcome the same. Yes, that means that the time continuum itself is kind of like a character here.<br />Due to the fact that the historians are working around the clock, suffering from time lag (yes, like jet lag, only worse), mistakes are made and slowly but surely we notice that the timeline (the net) is fighting back.<br />Ned, one of the historians, is suffering so much from time lag that he is sent to Victorian England for recovery (out of the way of Mrs. Schrapnell) just as a colleague of his has accidentally brought a cat forwards in time, supposedly violating the afore-mentioned time continuum law, which could even lead to the Nazis finding out about the Enigma having been compromised - which could, in turn, lead to a different outcome of WWII! <br />But history has a way of sorting itself out so of course he is smack in the middle of the continuum problem as well as contemporary romantic entanglements. To say nothing of the dog. ;)<br /><br />Connie Willis obviously loves Jerome K. Jerome's book <b>Three Men in a Boat</b> and has therefore incorporated the style as well as many plot elements of that book. She combines them with the style of other works, such as Agatha Christie’s and Arthur Conan Doyle’s crime novels or Shakespeare’s romantic comedies. However, she cleverly intertwines all of that with the time travel element and impresses once again with the amount of details, in this case mostly about the Victorian era. <br /><br />This combination makes this book much lighter and funnier than the first. Sure, the future of humanity is at stake, but we’re laughing along with the cat, are exasperated at the sheer stupidity of Tossie, and just enjoy the romp through all the misunderstandings in general. To say nothing of the time riddle and what really has happened. A very clever plot, I have to admit. In retrospect, the signs were indeed there, if one had only known where to look.<br /><br />Thus, while I was once again annoyed (especially about the fact that nobody just shut Mrs. Schrapnell into a closet, her money be damned) and although I would have wished for a bit more history (Victorian England is not as interesting to me as - for example - Ancient Rome), I enjoyed this much more than the first volume. Probably because I could actually laugh along with some of the people and liked both Princess Arjumand and Cyril (the cat and the dog) very much so I was thoroughly delighted that they played such central roles.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15940227782171329224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_764073162" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/764073162" />
    <a title="Kaethe Douglas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1072582-kaethe-douglas"><img alt="Kaethe Douglas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573927565p2/1072582.jpg" /></a>

<span id="freeTextContainer12632918587400013685">1998 Dec 21,<br/>1999 May 15<br/>1999 June 7<br/>2004 Apr 09<br/>2011 Nov 13<br/>2014 Jan 10<br/>2016 Jun 28<br/>2020 May 15<br/><br/>I read it again, and I loved it. This is definitely a comfort read for me. Ah, the madcap chaos of it all. The naughty cat, the charming Cyril, the annoying people. Total love.<br/><br/>***<br/>Speaking of books I love, down to every last detail, this is on the short list. The Chapter headings! The Tennyson quotes! The classism, sexism, and racism as viewed from a more enlightened viewpoint! The dog! The mystery novels of </span>
  <span id="freeText12632918587400013685" style="display:none">1998 Dec 21,<br />1999 May 15<br />1999 June 7<br />2004 Apr 09<br />2011 Nov 13<br />2014 Jan 10<br />2016 Jun 28<br />2020 May 15<br /><br />I read it again, and I loved it. This is definitely a comfort read for me. Ah, the madcap chaos of it all. The naughty cat, the charming Cyril, the annoying people. Total love.<br /><br />***<br />Speaking of books I love, down to every last detail, this is on the short list. The Chapter headings! The Tennyson quotes! The classism, sexism, and racism as viewed from a more enlightened viewpoint! The dog! The mystery novels of the Thirties! Time travel! Aggressive swans! Rowing on the Thames! Little screamlets! The sleep-deprived mental muddle which no one has ever described so well in print as Willis! Romance! Comedy! The horror of war!<br /><br />*** <br /><br />I own a copy that is spine-challenged and so worn from re-reading, even though I am a particularly gentle user of books. This is my idea of an uplifting story, with couples formed up at the end.<br /><br />***<br /><br />Two thoughts: it amuses me that I read this twice in less than a month, finishing on my birthday, and again on the birth day of my first born. Comfort in times of stress, indeed. The other is that Willis holds two superlatives in my book. She is the author who makes me cry the hardest ("Last of the Winnebagos", Doomsday Book, Passage) and also the one who makes me laugh the hardest (Bellwether, To Say Nothing of the Dog, and Crosstalk).<br /><br />And another thought: this may be the book I've read the most.<br /><br />***<br /><br />2020 May 15<br />At the moment this is the most-read full-length novel for me. Good Omens is behind by 1. Wonderful, but, so long. I'm now behind on my reading challenge. Time to squeeze in some picture books, I think. Or cartoons. Something light, anyway.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15096970039772877550">Most of this book was four stars for me but then it went and got <i>clever</i>. It ended up being completely brilliant and I got some excellent laughs out of it, especially the end.<br/><br/>Poor Ned Henry has a severe case of time lag and he's been to <i>way</i> too many jumble sales. He's taken nearly 15 trips back to the 1940s to find the Bishop's Bird Stump and he's having no luck.   <span id="freeText15096970039772877550" style="display:none">Most of this book was four stars for me but then it went and got <i>clever</i>. It ended up being completely brilliant and I got some excellent laughs out of it, especially the end.<br /><br />Poor Ned Henry has a severe case of time lag and he's been to <i>way</i> too many jumble sales. He's taken nearly 15 trips back to the 1940s to find the Bishop's Bird Stump and he's having no luck. Desperate to avoid Lady Schrapnell he goes to Mr. Dunworthy who promptly sends him back to the Victorian Era to have a bit of rest. He meets up with his contact... eventually... and then they spend most of the book frantically trying to make sure that Tossie marries Mr. C. to stop the space-time continuum from being destroyed.<br /><br />There are literary references galore and it creates an especially delightful homage to Agatha Christie and classic detective fiction. It was an extraordinarily fun book.<br /><br />This is my third book by Willis and considering that I've given them all 5 stars, I'm wondering why in the hell I'm not dropping everything to read more! She's brilliant <i>and</i> wonderfully funny.</span>

<div id="review_173114455" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/173114455" />
    <a title=" ~Geektastic~" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5006157-geektastic"><img alt=" ~Geektastic~" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605801004p2/5006157.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/173114455?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title=" ~Geektastic~" class="user" itemprop="url" name=" ~Geektastic~" href="/user/show/5006157-geektastic"> ~Geektastic~</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=currently-borrowed">currently-borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=gmc-2012">gmc-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5006157-geektastic?shelf=timey-wimey">timey-wimey</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer173114455" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7378988936431128900"> 4.5 stars, just to be clear.<br/><br/>Part time travel adventure, part comedy of manners and part mystery, <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> is a little bit of everything I love about books. <br/><br/><i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> takes its name (and much of its sensibility) from the famous novella by Jerome K. Jerome, <i>Three Men in a Boat, To Say Nothing of the Dog.</i> This choice is not incidental, but neither is it overwhelmingly important to the novel as a whole. The story is told through the eyes of Ned Henry, a time travell</span>
  <span id="freeText7378988936431128900" style="display:none"> 4.5 stars, just to be clear.<br /><br />Part time travel adventure, part comedy of manners and part mystery, <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> is a little bit of everything I love about books. <br /><br /><i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> takes its name (and much of its sensibility) from the famous novella by Jerome K. Jerome, <i>Three Men in a Boat, To Say Nothing of the Dog.</i> This choice is not incidental, but neither is it overwhelmingly important to the novel as a whole. The story is told through the eyes of Ned Henry, a time travelling historian in the not-too-distant future, as we witness his adventures into the late Victorian past. Poor Ned is overworked and just looking for a bit of a rest. His employer, the demanding and overbearing (and appropriately named) Lady Shrapnell, is looking to collect the various “treasures” that once resided in Coventry Cathedral in order to complete a restoration project, including a mysterious object known as “the bishop’s bird stump.” With no concern for the health of her staff or the laws of time, Shrapnell runs her team into the ground, and Ned into an advanced case of time-lag, the mind-muddling result of excessive time travel. Think jet lag but about 100 times worse and 1,000 times more entertaining. Prescribed two weeks of rest to recover but perpetually hounded by Lady Shrapnell, Ned is given the chance to travel back to the late Victorian countryside for a bit of R&amp;R. However, Ned is no expert in the chosen time, so naturally hilarity ensues. Of course, time travel is never so simple as a quick jaunt into the past, so Ned must confront a tangle of time related difficulties while also trying to maneuver through the intricacies of straight-laced Victorian society. <br /><br />Time travel is a notoriously difficult narrative tool to handle properly. Even the most phenomenally plotted story can collapse under the weight of disbelief a slip in mechanics can create. The key is to focus on the most important “rules” and to try to stick to them assiduously, while simultaneously avoiding the tendency to bog everything down in too much detail. Connie Willis has built a career and reputation on her ability to render time travel with just the right amount of detail and vagary to make her mechanics credible without overshadowing the plot with technical terms and excessive exposition. Granted, I’m making this statement based on this particular book and hearsay, but I stand by it. Willis’ time travel mechanics are simple, while their implications are much more complex. In <i>Dog</i>, the system is gradually revealed, rather than dumped on the reader first thing. This is beneficial in keeping things simple, but at the same time causes an occasional head scratching when an unexplained technical term is used. Everything is clarified in due time, however, so it’s not really any hindrance to the story. <br /><br />The heart of the story isn’t really the time travelling anyway. Most of the fun comes in the form of the characters Ned meets as he stumbles and blunders his way to a not-very-successful rest. A jaunt down the Thames soon introduces Ned to a myriad of famous Victorian types, including a poetry-spouting undergrad, an eccentric professor and a stiff-upper-lipped butler, to say nothing of the dog. Luckily for Ned, he also meets a fellow time traveler, the brilliant and beautiful Verity, who guides him through the social faux-pas minefield that was Victorian England. Verity is in 1888 on a mission, and when Ned becomes entangled as well, his rest goes right out the window and the trip becomes a mad dash to save all of time while still maintaining a stiff Victorian veneer.<br /><br />I really loved this book. It’s so difficult not to go on and on about every little fascinating reference to Victorian culture and literature, and every brilliant twist in the time travel narrative, but the enjoyment is in the gradual reveal of detail, so I don’t want to give anything away here. I will say that, while it’s not really necessary, you may enjoy this book even more if you read <i> Three Men in a Boat</i> first. Also, as much as I found to love in this book, I had some difficulty with Ned at first. As a narrator he’s well up to the task, but as a <i>character</i> he could have used a bit more fleshing out. We never really know what he looks like, or much about him at all, other than the fact that he is a historian and not very well versed in Victorian history. Essentially, Ned plays the straight man, observing and reflecting the comedy going on around him, so once this is accepted the story rolls along quite nicely. Now, in the finest tradition of “show, don’t tell,” we do learn that Ned is very capable and intelligent by observing his reactions and puzzle solving abilities, rather than hear him go on about himself, and I think this is what finally won me over in his favor.<br /><br />If you like time travel, P.G. Wodehouse, Victorian novels or any combination of the three, this would be the book for you.<br /><br />(<b>Warning</b>: If you have never read Wilkie Collins’ <i>The Moonstone</i> and plan on doing so, read it before picking this up. It gives the whole twist away. I know I shouldn’t be peeved about spoilers over 100 years old, but I was.)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7378988936431128900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_106341793" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106341793" />
    <a title="Althea Ann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3846743-althea-ann"><img alt="Althea Ann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411566466p2/3846743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/106341793?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Althea Ann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Althea Ann" href="/user/show/3846743-althea-ann">Althea Ann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer106341793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3467762339950347566">This book was a real stand-out for me! I picked it up after reading some good reviews, and I was pleasantly surprised to like it even better than I expected.<br/>It's a time-travel/mystery/romance which is consistently witty and often downright hilarious. Books which are simultaneously literary and humorous are hard to come by - but here, Willis succeeds amazingly well.<br/>In the near future, time travel has been discovered. It's being used by a wealthy society dame, Lady Schrapnell, in her well-funded p</span>
  <span id="freeText3467762339950347566" style="display:none">This book was a real stand-out for me! I picked it up after reading some good reviews, and I was pleasantly surprised to like it even better than I expected.<br />It's a time-travel/mystery/romance which is consistently witty and often downright hilarious. Books which are simultaneously literary and humorous are hard to come by - but here, Willis succeeds amazingly well.<br />In the near future, time travel has been discovered. It's being used by a wealthy society dame, Lady Schrapnell, in her well-funded pet project - to restore Coventry Cathedral, destryed in a WWII bombing raid. Her time-travelling agents live in fear of her harridan-like ways, especially Ned Henry, who's been assigned to ascertain exactly what happened to the Bishop's Bird Stump (a particularly grotesque and rococo piece of Victorian art).<br />Indirectly, this assignment takes him to Victorian England, where, affected by severe time-lag (think jet-lag x10) he ends up travelling down the river in the company of a recently-lovestruck young man - and a boisterous dog. The action picks up from there, with a cast of quirky characters, including the ditzy blonde Tossie, the pre-raphaelite beauty Verity, eccentric professors, prudish-and-proper parents, fraudulent psychics, and, of course, Princess Arjumand.<br />I haven't read "Three Men in a Boat, to Say Nothing of the Dog!" by Jerome K. Jerome - a genuine Victorian comedy that apparently inspired Willis stylistically - but I can say that this book would definitely appeal to any fans of Victorian fiction (experts in the field would, I'm sure, 'get' many things that I missed), as well as classic mystery fans (Arthur Conan Doyle and Agatha Christie are referenced more than once), and, of course, sci-fi/time travel aficionados. <br /><br />(I just noticed that I should probably note here, that after writing this (some time ago) I did get myself over to the library to read 'Three Men in A Boat': <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106341684">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>)</span>
  <a data-text-id="3467762339950347566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6448302828813011796">This is my absolute favorite book. A perfect blend of sci-fi, historical fiction, mystery, comedy, mistaken identity and romance; this book has it all. <br/><br/>Its the not-too-distant future, but time-travel has been around for awhile. Oxford historian Ned Henry is trying desperately to find a hideous Victorian object, the Bishop's Bird Stump, shuttling back and forth between World War Two and the Victorian Era. Meanwhile, another historian, Verity Kindle, accidentally brings something back from the pas</span>
  <span id="freeText6448302828813011796" style="display:none">This is my absolute favorite book. A perfect blend of sci-fi, historical fiction, mystery, comedy, mistaken identity and romance; this book has it all. <br /><br />Its the not-too-distant future, but time-travel has been around for awhile. Oxford historian Ned Henry is trying desperately to find a hideous Victorian object, the Bishop's Bird Stump, shuttling back and forth between World War Two and the Victorian Era. Meanwhile, another historian, Verity Kindle, accidentally brings something back from the past, potentially changing history and destroying time-travel. Ned (who only knows about the Victorian Era from Jerome K. Jerome's book, Three Men in a Boat to Say Nothing of the Dog) and Verity have to live in a ridiculous-and completely realistic-Victorian house, trying to help history correct itself but generally making things worse. <br /><br />Its clever, without the smug voice that tends to accompany clever novels. Towards the middle it gets a little slow, but if you keep going it picks right back up with a great finish. With great characters and intricate details, this is a superb novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6448302828813011796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
