<span id="freeTextContainer14793618002865831296">I've now read all of Connie Willis' Oxford Time Travel novels and I have to say that I found this one to be the best. The story was intriguing and funny and didn't have any of the ridiculous tension issues of the other books. No communication issues anywhere. No mistaken identities. Yes there were mysteries and things that needed to be resolved but they were handled in a way that made it enjoyable to read, not frustrating.<br/><br/>I look forward to seeing where she takes this series next. Highly recommen</span>
  <span id="freeText14793618002865831296" style="display:none">I've now read all of Connie Willis' Oxford Time Travel novels and I have to say that I found this one to be the best. The story was intriguing and funny and didn't have any of the ridiculous tension issues of the other books. No communication issues anywhere. No mistaken identities. Yes there were mysteries and things that needed to be resolved but they were handled in a way that made it enjoyable to read, not frustrating.<br /><br />I look forward to seeing where she takes this series next. Highly recommend this book.</span>

<div id="review_91797215" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91797215" />
    <a title="Lawyer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer"><img alt="Lawyer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603672493p2/3025547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/91797215?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lawyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lawyer" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer">Lawyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lawyer by: </span>
      A very good real life and goodreads friend
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=the-blitz">the-blitz</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=hugo-award-winner">hugo-award-winner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=locus-winner">locus-winner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=nebula-nominee">nebula-nominee</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer91797215" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6642698114155675888"><b>TO SAY NOTHING OF THE DOG, <i>or How I stopped worrying about the space-time continuum and learned to love discontinuity</i></b><br/><br/>This review about a novel concerning time travel is a bit of an exercise in time travel, itself. I had gone to add a book to my to-read shelf and there sat <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog' title='To Say Nothing of the Dog by Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>To Say Nothing of the Dog</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis' title='Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Connie Willis</a> as big as life. Now that can't be right, I thought. I read this in 2010. I loved this book. I'm sure I even reviewed it. I thought. Therefore, there are no read dates I can assign to t</span>
  <span id="freeText6642698114155675888" style="display:none"><b>TO SAY NOTHING OF THE DOG, <i>or How I stopped worrying about the space-time continuum and learned to love discontinuity</i></b><br /><br />This review about a novel concerning time travel is a bit of an exercise in time travel, itself. I had gone to add a book to my to-read shelf and there sat <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog" title="To Say Nothing of the Dog by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">To Say Nothing of the Dog</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" title="Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Connie Willis</a> as big as life. Now that can't be right, I thought. I read this in 2010. I loved this book. I'm sure I even reviewed it. I thought. Therefore, there are no read dates I can assign to this, other than it was early 2010, either January, or February. Well, well. Almost two years. How time flies. <br /><br />Call it a facet of the aging process. That's a phrase my wife taught me after a fall she had. I did tell her not to wear those shoes, clunky platform sandals, that she would catch the sole on something or other and that she would hit the dirt. And she did, going to get her new car tag, which an officer had been so kind to remind her was, oh, expired by about a year.<br /><br />I'm sitting in <i>my</i> doctor's office when I get a call on my cell from a strange man asking are you married to...yep, my wife. "Uh-huh." There's a bit of silence on the other end of the phone, then he blurts, "Well, she's fallen in front of the courthouse annex and she looks pretty bad." <br /><br />"Uhm, Doc, how bout you check out your next patient, MJ fell and I gotta go get her. We'll be back. You'll get a twofer today."<br /><br />So, up to the annex where I know everybody from being a courthouse regular. A lady looks up, "Oh. She's in the back." I skip around the corner. There's my wife wearing those clunky platform sandals with a bag of ice over her face. I gingerly pulled it away and winced.<br /><br />"Dey were bery dice. I got by dag." She said, pointing to the tag in her lap.<br /><br />"Uh-huh. Come on, we're going back to the Doc's. Thanks everybody for taking care of her." She had caught the sole of her shoe on the curb and slammed face first onto the sidewalk. In a way, it was where the sidewalk ended--for her that day. Oh, that was bad. She told me so on the way to the Doc's.<br /><br />Back at the Doc's ranch, things were jumping. The waiting room was full. I signed in again and signed in my wife as well. About this time she's tapping me on the shoulder and announces: "And ib ooo eber boo anyding lige dad agin, I'b galling de bolice."<br /><br />You could have heard a pin drop. Then she gives this goofy grin and says "I was only kidding." The receptionist rescued me and said she really was, that I had told her as I blew by her on the way out that I was going to pick my wife up who had fallen. AND...He's the director of the domestic violence shelter.<br /><br />There was a mass sigh of relief. I could hear my pulse diminishing in my ears.<br /><br />Only, after the Doc patched her up, MJ would be in mid-sentence and wouldn't be able to come up with a word, like, dog for instance. So the Doc does a referral to a neurologist who ultimately says she has a visceral loop, it's ok, and everything should be fine, to say nothing about not being able to say dog.<br /><br />I overheard MJ calling her brother when we got home. She was explaining her diagnosis, saying, "I have a...vaginal loop." I can hear her brother's loud "WHAT?" from across the room, got on the other extension and explained things.<br /><br />Everything was fine. My wife got all her words back, particularly when issuing opinions, suggestions, instructions, imprimaturs, and so on.<br /><br />So, I must have had some kind of visceral loop (Is that a real diagnosis? I'm thinking she substituted visceral for something else that started with a vee. I know it wasn't virginity. Wait, wait--it was venous!) when I forgot to shelve and review this great book.<br /><br />And you were wondering when we would get to this point. We're here. Think of it as traveling with children. After all the "When are we gonna get theres," you arrive at your destination.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14032.Connie_Willis" title="Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Connie Willis</a>is one of my favorite speculative fiction writers. I say speculative as opposed to science fiction, not as a disparagement to science fiction, but because there's not a lot of science in this book. It is your basic time travel story, complete with the classic time travel paradox, i.e. don't change anything, you'll screw everything back in the present to heck and gone.<br /><br />Willis won the Hugo and Locus Awards for Best Science Fiction (I don't believe they have awards for speculative fiction, do they?) in 1998. She was nominated for the 1999 Nebula Award, but <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12476.Joe_Haldeman" title="Joe Haldeman" rel="nofollow noopener">Joe Haldeman</a>won for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21618.Forever_Peace" title="Forever Peace by Joe Haldeman" rel="nofollow noopener">Forever Peace</a>. Well, you can't win 'em all. However, she has won eleven Hugos, Seven Nebulas, four Locus Awards, and the John W. Campbell Award for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24980.Lincoln_s_Dreams" title="Lincoln's Dreams by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Lincoln's Dreams</a> back in 1988. In short, she's damned good.<br /><br />Willis' best recognized works are set in Oxford in the 21st Century. Historians are constantly sent back in time to make sure things are going along swimmingly and to conduct historical research to see what really happened. After all, as we all know, history is written by the victors. Most of the "drops" back in time occur during World War II, focusing on the time of the Blitz, as something happened to really wreck the time continuum back then. Her other blockbuster, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book" title="Doomsday Book by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Doomsday Book</a>focused on a drop to the 14th century. Can you say "Bubonic Plague?" I know you can!<br /><br />One of the features of Willis' time travel novels that especially appeals to me are her takes on the foibles of humanity. During the most dire of circumstances you will find some character or characters focusing on the most inconsequential matter while the world is falling apart around their ears. An example would be the Bell Ringers who are bent and determined on performing a bell performance at Christmas, although the plague has been brought to 2057 quite by accident.<br /><br />A word about the title: Willis dedicated <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog" title="To Say Nothing of the Dog by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">To Say Nothing of the Dog</a> to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5166604.Robert_HEINLEIN" title="Robert HEINLEIN" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert HEINLEIN</a> for introducing her to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/627830.Three_Men_in_a_Boat_To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog__Tor_Classics_" title="Three Men in a Boat To Say Nothing of the Dog (Tor Classics) by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men in a Boat: To Say Nothing of the Dog</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3352.Jerome_K__Jerome" title="Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Jerome K. Jerome</a>, published in 1889. It is a comic tale of a boating holiday on the Thames. The boating trip occurred. However, the dog, Montmorency, was completely fictional.<br /><br />"To Say Nothing of the Dog" is the perfect title. Willis deals with two drops back into time. One, to 1889, to return something which a historian erroneously brought back from that time and the other being to a Cathedral that existed in Coventry which was bombed to smithereens by the Luftwaffe. <br /><br />Returning the item to 1889 is a real problem, because the only historian available to take the item back is poor Ned Henry, a specialist in 20th century history. He knows NOTHING about the 1880's. Further, he's made so many drops of late, he's developed time lag. By the time he gets to 1889, he's forgotten his destination and what he was to return. Well, here's a pretty howdy-do. Of course, who should he run into but Jerome K. Jerome and his boating pals. Comedy ensues.<br /><br />In the meanwhile, Lady Schrapnell, who is wealthier than any human deserves, has determined that she WILL have Coventry Cathedral restored just as it existed before it was bombed during WWII. Almost every historian has been assigned to the task of dropping back to make sure that things are done correctly. There's one slight problem. There was the "Bishop's Bird Stump," which seems to have gone missing. The problem is no one knows what the heck it looked like.<br /><br />Historian Verity Kindle who specializes in 1930's mystery fiction is sent back to read one Tossie Mering's diary, Tossie, an ancestor of Lady Schrapnell spoke of an event which caused her to elope with "Mr. C. who believes it may contain a clue as to what happened to the bird stump and what it looked like.<br /><br />Verity complicated things by unwittingly bringing Tossie's Cat back to 2057 where cats have become extinct. Prince Armujand, yes, that's his name, cute as he is, has to go back. That's a darned shame because everybody in 2057 at the time continuum project wants one.<br /><br />Will Ned remember what he's returning and where it belongs? Will Tossie be united with Prince? Will Tossie elope to America with the stranger, Mr. C? And just what the heck was that Bishop's Bird Stump?<br /><br />Think of Wilde's "The Importance of Being Earnest." Mix well with "It's a Mad, Mad, Mad, Mad World, toss with H.G. Well's "The Time Machine," and serve immediately.<br /><br />I won't tell you about the Bishop's Bird Stump, but it couldn't have been uglier than those clunky shoes of my wife. Of course, Victorian bric a brac could be so darned gauche.<br /><br />Time travel? Check. Adventure? Check. Romance? Check, check. Comedy of errors? Check, check, check. Comedy of Manners? Are you kidding me? People NEVER change! Time Travel? Oh, yeah. Puppies and Kitties? Got them, too.<br /><br /><b>Rating: 4.5</b> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6642698114155675888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1464264461" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1464264461" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7022024-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470679918p2/7022024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1464264461?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/7022024-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8194647-to-say-nothing-of-the-dog">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1464264461" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10483200607782731978">So this was a fun little read, with elements of sci-fi time travel, a Victorian era social comedy story, and a 1930's mystery with a very light touch of romance thrown in.<br/><br/>In the future we have mastered the ability to travel through time... only to discover we can't actually bring anything consequential back (sorry people who want to steal the Mona Lisa fresh off da Vinci's easel) and any attempts to change key events are thwarted by the space-time continuum itself. In this way is was a bit like </span>
  <span id="freeText10483200607782731978" style="display:none">So this was a fun little read, with elements of sci-fi time travel, a Victorian era social comedy story, and a 1930's mystery with a very light touch of romance thrown in.<br /><br />In the future we have mastered the ability to travel through time... only to discover we can't actually bring anything consequential back (sorry people who want to steal the Mona Lisa fresh off da Vinci's easel) and any attempts to change key events are thwarted by the space-time continuum itself. In this way is was a bit like Stephen King's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/325827993?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">11/22/63</a>, with coincidences and alteration of time jumps preserving the general thrust of history. Of course that doesn't stop us from studying the past, but there isn't much money in that especially when time travelers can't even get close to the good stuff since the continuum won't risk many changes. This, as you can imagine, leaves funds for time travel a bit difficult to get. <br /><br />Of course this just makes universities and time travel organizations desperate enough to offer their talents to the super wealthy, in this case a rich American who is obsessed the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coventry_Cathedral" rel="nofollow noopener">Coventry Cathedral</a> which had a major impact on the life of an ancestor of hers. She decides to spend lavishly to create a perfect reconstruction of it, down to every insignificant detail and harangues the time travel agencies to send their workers on time travel drops of every conceivable kind to get every little detail perfect.<br /><br />The book opens with the main character and time travel historian Ned Henry in a bad spot, stuck on a seemingly impossible mission to determine what has happened to the Bishop's Bird Stump, a historic artifact that has yet to be located fro the reconsecration of the Coventry Cathedral. He is also suffering heavily from time-lag, a by product of too frequent time travel which affects the mind in a similar manner as extreme fatigue. He then finds himself on a bit of a misadventure as he is sent back to the Victorian era for an easy mission (which he does not remember) to meet another agent (whom he does not recall) and then spend the rest of the time relaxing.<br /><br />Sufficed to say nothing goes as planned. He is still completely time lagged and has no idea what he is doing. He falls with a bunch of very colorful characters right out of a Victorian story: the matron obsessed with the spirits, a patron who loves his fish and finds spiritualism to be a bunch of hooey, the absent minded Oxford Don, the hyper efficient but constantly put upon butler, the romantic and poetry spouting undergrad who falls in love with the spoiled and pampered daughter of the aforementioned patron and matron, to say nothing of the dog. Ned and his fellow agent Verity (who he does meet up with) try to unravel all the many accidents and problems they inadvertently cause in the time line while navigating this tangled social web. <br /><br />This was for the most part a really fun read. The characters were suitable absurd that I would often find myself laughing out loud at their antics. Willis does an excellent job balancing the time travel plot and the quest for the Bishop's Bird Stump with the Victorian era events that Ned and Verity end up mucking up a bit. By the end we discover the mystery behind all the tricks the continuum has been playing on the time travelers and just what happened to that wretched bird stump (a solution found using nothing but Ned's "little gray cells" as Poirot would say). I found the conclusion of the book quite satisfying with everything wrapped up nicely and logically.<br /><br />While I did enjoy the book a lot I thought the beginning and ending dragged a bit. The beginning was a bit slow and then Ned gets hit with full blown time-lag so what was going on around him wasn't terribly clear. Things didn't really get going until he ends up on the Thames in the 19th century. The ending felt a bit rushed and cluttered but it did get the important points across<br /><br />All in all well worth reading as the absurd characters, great writing, and time travel mystery really kept me engaged. Also, while this is the second book in the Oxford Time Travel Series, it is a stand alone book that doesn't require knowledge of the first to enjoy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10483200607782731978" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Even when Willis writes about the more annoying characters, it's with such bonhomie they become like irritating family members that you hope will leave soon but they are still family so you're stuck with them, and after they leave you can have a good laugh and roll your eyes at their antics. And I miss Cyril and Princess Amahajumed the most, I wish </span>
  <span id="freeText6696882117040926276" style="display:none">Ah, I was so bummed when this book was over, I would have gladly stayed with these characters for at least a month, that's how delightful they were. Even when Willis writes about the more annoying characters, it's with such bonhomie they become like irritating family members that you hope will leave soon but they are still family so you're stuck with them, and after they leave you can have a good laugh and roll your eyes at their antics. And I miss Cyril and Princess Amahajumed the most, I wish I could take them home with me!<br /><br />Another great one from Connie Willis. Much lighter of heart than Doomsday and Passage. The only reason I'm not giving this a 5 is because I also just finished Incredibly Close and Extremely Loud, which I am floored by and think is a Masterpiece. Timing is everything!</span>

<div id="review_153452980" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/153452980" />
    <a title="Corinna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/570383-corinna"><img alt="Corinna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1297975795p2/570383.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/153452980?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Corinna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Corinna" href="/user/show/570383-corinna">Corinna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Corinna by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3533898-phil-gonzales">Phil Gonzales</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer153452980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17033523878558546886">Many people know that <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77225.Three_Men_in_a_Boat_to_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog_' title='Three Men in a Boat to Say Nothing of the Dog! by Jerome K. Jerome' rel='nofollow noopener'>Three Men in a Boat: to Say Nothing of the Dog!</a> is probably my favorite book. What many people don't necessarily know is that I first read it because I bought a very old copy of it at a book sale, and the reason I bought it was because I had read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20417.Have_Space_Suit_Will_Travel' title='Have Space Suit-Will Travel by Robert A. Heinlein' rel='nofollow noopener'>Have Space Suit-Will Travel</a> by Robert A. Heinlein, (to whom <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog' title='To Say Nothing of the Dog by Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>To Say Nothing of the Dog</a>is dedicated) in which the main character, Kip, interrupts his father as he is reading HIS favorite book, Three Men in a Boat, in which, he claims</span>
  <span id="freeText17033523878558546886" style="display:none">Many people know that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77225.Three_Men_in_a_Boat_to_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog_" title="Three Men in a Boat to Say Nothing of the Dog! by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men in a Boat: to Say Nothing of the Dog!</a> is probably my favorite book. What many people don't necessarily know is that I first read it because I bought a very old copy of it at a book sale, and the reason I bought it was because I had read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20417.Have_Space_Suit_Will_Travel" title="Have Space Suit-Will Travel by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">Have Space Suit-Will Travel</a> by Robert A. Heinlein, (to whom <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog" title="To Say Nothing of the Dog by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">To Say Nothing of the Dog</a>is dedicated) in which the main character, Kip, interrupts his father as he is reading HIS favorite book, Three Men in a Boat, in which, he claims, all of life's lessons can be found.<br /><br />I think <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77773.To_Say_Nothing_of_the_Dog" title="To Say Nothing of the Dog by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">To Say Nothing of the Dog</a> would be fine to read if you'd never read any of Jerome K. Jerome's writing, but I think a familiarity with his work makes this book even more fun to read. The little summaries of action at the start of each chapter, the many references to Jerome's book (and even his person!) during the first part of the book, and even to some degree the measured, deliberate pacing of the book all stand as tribute to <u>Three Men in a Boat: To Say Nothing of the Dog</u>.<br /><br />Of course this book does have it's own wacky men, it's own wacky (if short-lived) boat trip and it's own dog with a unique personality (albeit calmer than that of Montmorency!). I also appreciated that there is a cat who is quite a pivotal character - I take this to be another wink at the Heinlein fans reading - that man had such a thing for cats in his writing.<br /><br />As for the story itself, there is a great deal of time travel, which can be a head-scratcher, and there are some unique rules for it in this book, but overall, I thought it was highly interesting and enjoyable. I might need to read it again to really get all the twists and turns, however. I did get annoyed by the main characters' total inability to discern who Mr. C was, but I think that we were perhaps meant to figure that out well ahead of the protagonists. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17033523878558546886" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_764081997" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/764081997" />
    <a title="Wanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda"><img alt="Wanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528903039p2/9246169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/764081997?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wanda" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda">Wanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=reading-project">reading-project</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=2020-reading-list">2020-reading-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=good-dog">good-dog</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=z-1990s">z-1990s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=female-authors">female-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=public-library">public-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=november-2020">november-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer764081997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4998129267406738960">Well, that was fun. A wonderful time travel whodunnit. I forget where I read that this book was informed by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat__Three_Men___1_' title='Three Men in a Boat (Three Men, #1) by Jerome K. Jerome' rel='nofollow noopener'>Three Men in a Boat</a>, which I read earlier this year in preparation for reading this novel. Although I didn't find Jerome K. Jerome's tale too engaging, I loved Connie Willis' version. I’m so glad I was familiar with Mr. Jerome's work, as it provided a great deal of humour. Although this novel features the same university department as Willis' <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book__Oxford_Time_Travel___1_' title='Doomsday Book (Oxford Time Travel, #1) by Connie Willis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Doomsday Book</a>, it had an entirely different ton</span>
  <span id="freeText4998129267406738960" style="display:none">Well, that was fun. A wonderful time travel whodunnit. I forget where I read that this book was informed by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat__Three_Men___1_" title="Three Men in a Boat (Three Men, #1) by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Men in a Boat</a>, which I read earlier this year in preparation for reading this novel. Although I didn't find Jerome K. Jerome's tale too engaging, I loved Connie Willis' version. I’m so glad I was familiar with Mr. Jerome's work, as it provided a great deal of humour. Although this novel features the same university department as Willis' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book__Oxford_Time_Travel___1_" title="Doomsday Book (Oxford Time Travel, #1) by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Doomsday Book</a>, it had an entirely different tone. This one is practically slapstick by comparison.<br /><br />Ms. Willis obviously shares some literary interests with me. I loved the references to Conan Doyle &amp; Sherlock Holmes, Agatha Christie &amp; Poirot, Dorothy Sayers &amp; Lord Peter Wimsey &amp; Harriet Vane, P.G. Wodehouse &amp; Jeeves &amp; Wooster, not to mention Wilkie Collins' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6138.The_Moonstone" title="The Moonstone by Wilkie Collins" rel="nofollow noopener">The Moonstone</a>. This kind of thing is just catnip to me. So many of my favourites alluded to all in one place. Not to mention the sighting of Jerome out on the river!<br /><br />Willis also has a talent for names—what else do you call the rich, pushy woman who has taken over everything and destroyed the regular work of the history department? Why, Lady Schrapnell, of course! Perfect. I think that Jodi Taylor must have read this before writing her own time travel romp <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29661618.Just_One_Damned_Thing_After_Another__The_Chronicles_of_St_Mary_s___1_" title="Just One Damned Thing After Another (The Chronicles of St Mary's, #1) by Jodi Taylor" rel="nofollow noopener">Just One Damned Thing After Another</a>, another book that I've enjoyed a lot.<br /><br />It took me a couple of attempts to really get going in this book just because of life matters, but once I reached a certain point, I couldn't set it down until I was finished. <br /><br /><b>Book number 382 of my Science Fiction &amp; Fantasy Reading Project.</b><br /><br />Cross posted at my blog:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://wanda-thenextfifty.blogspot.com/2020/11/to-say-nothing-of-dog-connie-willis.html">https://wanda-thenextfifty.blogspot.c...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4998129267406738960" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer78818488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2658034812147514524">All of Connie Willis' other time travel books are too long. This one is too short.<br/><br/>That said, I think it is incredibly cruel to create a future timeline with no cats in it. I have seen your future, and I do not want to live in it.<br/><br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[Good thing the space-time continuum apparently abhors a vacuum of kittens. <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br/><br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[Wow, how often to you manage to cram two "uu" words into the same sentence? <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <span id="freeText2658034812147514524" style="display:none">All of Connie Willis' other time travel books are too long. This one is too short.<br /><br />That said, I think it is incredibly cruel to create a future timeline with no cats in it. <div id="review_585670353" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/585670353" />
    <a title="Alexis Hall" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19022440-alexis-hall"><img alt="Alexis Hall" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533751057p2/19022440.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/585670353?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexis Hall" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexis Hall" href="/user/show/19022440-alexis-hall">Alexis Hall</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19022440-alexis-hall?shelf=sci-fi-and-or-fantasy">sci-fi-and-or-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19022440-alexis-hall?shelf=books-i-would-save-before-my-cat">books-i-would-save-before-my-cat</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19022440-alexis-hall?shelf=a-few-of-my-favourite-things">a-few-of-my-favourite-things</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19022440-alexis-hall?shelf=i-doff-my-hat-to-your-cleverness">i-doff-my-hat-to-your-cleverness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19022440-alexis-hall?shelf=delightful">delightful</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer585670353" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6461304212175798567">This book is the best book.<br/><br/>That is all.<br/><br/>Okay maybe it's not all.<br/><br/>I don't even know how to describe it ... a ridiculously clever, ridiculously funny madcap time-travelling Victoriana-infused romp?<br/><br/>Profoundly Oxfordy as well - full of references and allusions that make me at once feel at one and in on a snooty private joke. Which is a feeling I kinda enjoy. Because I'm a bad person.<br/><br/>Also Lady Shrapnel is the best name for a villain.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Qynd9IfxDr77BHWyOxrSRQtWvFTDbv6IzHPqZ3CrAPR5D7QmPK/H1lwvwdPM79ia3QlbnA3jcO+XDazWvejybg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_4921'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Three Men in a Boat (Three Men, #1)<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/3352.Jerome_K_Jerome?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Jerome K. Jerome<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.88 avg rating &mdash; 56,140 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1889\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer16321469373358282095\">A comic masterpiece that has never been out of print since it was first published in 1889, Jerome K. Jerome\'s Three Men in a Boat includes an introduction and notes by Jeremy Lewis in Penguin Classics.\n\nMartyrs to hypochondria and general seediness, <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16321469373358282095\" style=\"display:none\">A comic masterpiece that has never been out of print since it was first published in 1889, Jerome K. Jerome\'s Three Men in a Boat includes an introduction and notes by Jeremy Lewis in Penguin Classics.\n\nMartyrs to hypochondria and general seediness, J. and his friends George and Harris decide that a jaunt up the Thames would suit them to a \'T\'. But when they set off, they can hardly predict the troubles that lie ahead with tow-ropes, unreliable weather forecasts and tins of pineapple chunks - not to mention the devastation left in the wake of J.\'s small fox-terrier Montmorency. I\'m Right, You\'re Wrong, Now What is based on Dr. Amador\'s LISTEN-EMPATHIZE-AGREE-PARTNER (LEAP) method. A highly successful program that has been taught to tens of thousands of people in seminars around the U.S. and overseas, LEAP teaches you how to turn even toxic arguments into healthy disagreements that end with you getting what you need. Built on timeless psychological truths and new research, LEAP is a roadmap for improving the quality and health of any relationship. LEAP will actually show you how to convince the other person to help you, while increasing mutual respect and trust. Perhaps most importantly, it will help you make that all-important distinction between what you want and what you need. Dr. Amador\'s LEAP program includes techniques on how to:\ndiffuse anger and lower defenses\nget past stubbornness and even denial\nmake your opponent ask for your opinion . . . instead of railing against it\nturn adversaries into allies\ncreate positive and productive relationships\n At home, at work and in life, LEAP demonstrates how winning is not about hearing the other person say Could something be wrong with the way mathematics is taught?\n\nFollowing his years of studying human intellectual accomplishments such as language, reading, writing, thinking, and learning, Frank Smith now turns his critical lens on the teaching and learning of mathematics. In The Glass Wall, Smith helps us to understand why some people find the world of mathematics so compelling while others find it so difficult. This original volume examines two different worlds: the physical world (our familiar world of objects and events) and the world of mathematics (a completely different domain of experience), and the glass wall that can exist between them. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14077945578863313924">Constance Elaine Trimmer Willis is an American science fiction writer. She is one of the most honored science fiction writers of the 1980s and 1990s.<br/><br/>She has won, among other awards, ten Hugo Awards and six Nebula Awards. Willis most recently won a Hugo Award for All Seated on the Ground (August 2008). She was the 2011 recipient of the Damon Knight Memorial Grand Master Award from the Science Ficti</span>
  <span id="freeText14077945578863313924" style="display:none">Constance Elaine Trimmer Willis is an American science fiction writer. She is one of the most honored science fiction writers of the 1980s and 1990s.<br /><br />She has won, among other awards, ten Hugo Awards and six Nebula Awards. Willis most recently won a Hugo Award for All Seated on the Ground (August 2008). She was the 2011 recipient of the Damon Knight Memorial Grand Master Award from the Science Fiction Writers of America (SFWA).<br /><br />She lives in Greeley, Colorado with her husband Courtney Willis, a professor of physics at the University of Northern Colorado. She also has one daughter, Cordelia.<br /><br />Willis is known for her accessible prose and likable characters. She has written several pieces involving time travel by history students and faculty of the future University of Oxford. These pieces include her Hugo Award-winning novels <i>Doomsday Book</i> and <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> and the short story "Fire Watch," found in the short story collection of the same name.<br /><br />Willis tends to the comedy of manners style of writing. Her protagonists are typically beset by single-minded people pursuing illogical agendas, such as attempting to organize a bell-ringing session in the middle of a deadly epidemic (<i>Doomsday Book</i>), or frustrating efforts to analyze near-death experiences by putting words in the mouths of interviewees (<i>Passage</i>).</span>
  <a data-text-id="14077945578863313924" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

But when the three become unexpectedly trapped in 1940, they struggle not only to find their way home but to survive as Hitler’s bombers attempt to pummel London into submission. Now the situation has grown even more dire. Small discrepancies in the historical record seem to indicate that one or all of them have somehow affected the past, changing the outcome of the war. The belief that the past can be observed but never altered has always been a core belief of time-travel theory—but suddenly it seems that the theory is horribly, tragically wrong. Meanwhile, in 2060 Oxford, the historians’ supervisor, Mr. Dunworthy, and seventeen-year-old Colin Templer, who nurses a powerful crush on Polly, are engaged in a frantic and seemingly impossible struggle of their own—to find three missing needles in the haystack of history. Told with compassion, humor, and an artistry both uplifting and devastating, All Clear is more than just the triumphant culmination of the adventure that began with Blackout. It’s Connie Willis’s most humane, heartfelt novel yet—a clear-eyed celebration of faith, love, and the quiet, ordinary acts of heroism and sacrifice too often overlooked by history.   <\/span>\n  <a
